<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/templates/jsp/pb2/js/plugin/thiss-ds.js" nonce="6cc87e59834c4b45b839acf33882a6bf"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="6cc87e59834c4b45b839acf33882a6bf">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '8590389a-30e0-4a00-b11b-7dba9c72c6a8'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

            function riskifiedBeaconLoad() {

Lavrentiev ave., Novosibirsk, 630090 Russia</p><a class="moreInfoLink" href="/authored-by/Zaguzina/Alena+A.">Search for more papers by this author</a></div></span><span class="accordion-tabbed__tab-mobile  accordion__closed"><a href="/authored-by/Fedorenko/Anastasiya+D." class="author-name accordion-tabbed__control" data-id="a3" data-db-target-for="a3" aria-controls="a3" aria-haspopup="true" id="a3_Ctrl" role="button"><span>Anastasiya D.    <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d128824303" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            <p>The development of efficient anodes for sodium-ion batteries requires a deep understanding of the electrochemical processes involved. Herein, X-ray spectroscopy is used to study changes in the electronic structure of MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets coupled with few-layered graphene at early stages of battery life. Working electrode is combined with a sodium plate and a solution of NaClO<sub>4</sub> in ethyl carbonate/dimethyl carbonate in a homemade cell having an X-ray transparent window. X-ray absorption spectra at the S K-edge and Mo L<sub>3</sub>-edge are recorded using a synchrotron source, X-ray emission S Kα and Mo Lα spectra are obtained on a laboratory spectrometer for initial powder and electrode material at different potentials of the cell. Analysis of the spectra shows the occupation of the initially empty orbitals of MoS<sub>2</sub> by sodium electrons as a result of the interaction of the components of the sodium-ion half-cell. Ex situ X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy measurements of a fully discharged electrode material reveal partial transformation of hexagonal MoS<sub>2</sub> to disordered tetragonal MoS<sub>2</sub> coated with a surface electrolyte interphase layer. The obtained results indicate that coupling MoS<sub>2</sub> to graphene prevents the breaking of Mo–S bonds when Na<sup>+</sup> ions are first introduced, which may promote stable battery performance.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="pssb202400272-sec-0001">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="pssb202400272-sec-0001-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) share the same energy storage principles as the widely used lithium-ion batteries (LIBs), and due to significantly lower cost, SIBs are expected to be in high demand in electric vehicles and large grid applications.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#pssb202400272-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="pssb202400272-bib-0001_R_d128824295e329" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Engineering">1</a>, <a href="#pssb202400272-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="pssb202400272-bib-0002_R_d128824295e329" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - J. Phys. Energy">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Batteries are a complex system consisting of three main components: anode, cathode, and electrolyte, contained between the electrodes. Most research is currently focused on finding efficient anode materials for SIBs since the graphite used in commercial LIBs does not intercalate sodium well.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#pssb202400272-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="pssb202400272-bib-0003_R_d128824295e334" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Adv. Energy. Mater.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Molybdenum disulfide (MoS<sub>2</sub>) is one of the actively studied anode materials; it has a layered structure like graphite and a large interlayer distance of ≈0.62 nm, suitable for the accommodation of sodium ions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#pssb202400272-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="pssb202400272-bib-0004_R_d128824295e342" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Energy Storage Mater.">4</a>, <a href="#pssb202400272-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="pssb202400272-bib-0005_R_d128824295e342" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - ChemSusChem">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> When the interlayer space contains more than 1.5 Na per unit of MoS<sub>2</sub>, the intercalate decomposes with the formation of Mo and Na<sub>2</sub>S.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#pssb202400272-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="pssb202400272-bib-0006_R_d128824295e351" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - ACS Nano">6</a>, <a href="#pssb202400272-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="pssb202400272-bib-0007_R_d128824295e351" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Nano Energy">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This conversion reaction is expected to be irreversible due to the strong Na–S bonding.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#pssb202400272-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="pssb202400272-bib-0008_R_d128824295e357" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - J. Mater. Chem. A">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, experimental studies show that although MoS<sub>2</sub> does not restore its original structure, the Mo–S bonds are identified after sodium extraction.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#pssb202400272-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="pssb202400272-bib-0009_R_d128824295e364" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Energy Storage Mater.">9</a>, <a href="#pssb202400272-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="pssb202400272-bib-0010_R_d128824295e364" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The mechanism of transformation of MoS<sub>2</sub> during its electrochemical interaction with sodium ions has also not been fully understood. Thus, monitoring the reaction by electron paramagnetic resonance (EPR) showed that the adsorption/desorption of Na<sup>+</sup> ions occurs on Mo<sup>5+</sup> defects.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#pssb202400272-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="pssb202400272-bib-0011_R_d128824295e376" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Chem. Mater.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In situ electron diffraction observed a set of intermediate phases up to the Na<sub>1.75</sub>MoS<sub>2</sub> stoichiometry.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#pssb202400272-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="pssb202400272-bib-0007_R_d128824295e386" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Nano Energy">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> A study of the products of deep sodiation of MoS<sub>2</sub> few-layer sheets revealed the disordered MoS<sub>x</sub> clusters<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#pssb202400272-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="pssb202400272-bib-0010_R_d128824295e395" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and the coexistence of Mo<sub>6</sub>S<sub>8</sub>, Na<sub>2</sub>S, and Na<sub>2</sub>S<sub>2</sub> species.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#pssb202400272-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="pssb202400272-bib-0009_R_d128824295e412" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Energy Storage Mater.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It is obvious that electrochemical processes can be influenced by defects in the MoS<sub>2</sub> lattice, the presence of additives (for example, a carbon component), and the architecture of the active material.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#pssb202400272-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="pssb202400272-bib-0012_R_d128824295e419" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Nanomaterials">12</a>, <a href="#pssb202400272-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="pssb202400272-bib-0013_R_d128824295e419" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>The development of materials for high-performance batteries requires a deep understanding of the interface interactions between cell components and the processes that occur during the various stages of a battery's life. These studies can be conducted using in situ and operando experiments. X-ray spectroscopy techniques have certain advantages since they examine the electronic state of a specific element and provide information about the local atomic structure.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#pssb202400272-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="pssb202400272-bib-0014_R_d128824295e426" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Energy Storage Mater.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> X-ray absorption near-edge structure (XANES) spectroscopy is especially attractive for studying MoS<sub>2</sub> samples because the S K-edge and Mo L<sub>3</sub>-edge have close absorption energies and therefore the corresponding spectra can be measured simultaneously.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#pssb202400272-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="pssb202400272-bib-0015_R_d128824295e435" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - J. Phys. Chem. C">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> These measurements are carried out using synchrotron radiation sources, which allow rapid collection of spectroscopic data and provide high-energy resolution.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#pssb202400272-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="pssb202400272-bib-0016_R_d128824295e441" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Batter. Supercaps.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To the best of our knowledge, there are only two XANES studies on sodiation/desodiation of MoS<sub>2</sub> samples.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#pssb202400272-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="pssb202400272-bib-0009_R_d128824295e448" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Energy Storage Mater.">9</a>, <a href="#pssb202400272-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="pssb202400272-bib-0017_R_d128824295e448" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - J. Alloys Compd.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Operando experiments were carried out using a cell containing MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets, a sodium counter electrode (i.e., in half-cell), and an electrolyte of 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub> in diglyme.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#pssb202400272-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="pssb202400272-bib-0009_R_d128824295e458" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Energy Storage Mater.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> At the end of the first discharge process, a Na<sub>x</sub>Mo<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub>-type phase was discovered, which was assumed to provide high diffusion of sodium ions. Ex situ studies of deeply sodiated commercial MoS<sub>2</sub> powder revealed reduction of molybdenum to the metallic state.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#pssb202400272-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="pssb202400272-bib-0017_R_d128824295e472" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - J. Alloys Compd.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>In this work, we study the influence of the carbon component of a MoS<sub>2</sub>-based nanomaterial on its interaction with sodium ions. The nanomaterial was synthesized by simultaneous rapid thermal decomposition of a mixture of ammonium tetrathiomolybdate ((NH<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>MoS<sub>4</sub>) and graphene oxide (GO), resulting in the coupling of MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets with reduced GO (rGO). To perform in situ and operando measurements in high vacuum, we developed a custom cell using commercial CR2032 coin parts. The MoS<sub>2</sub>/rGO-based electrode was combined with a sodium plate and NaClO<sub>4</sub> electrolyte and examined by XANES and X-ray emission spectroscopy (XES) in a freshly assembled cell, after storage at open circuit potential, and during the first discharge. XANES probes unoccupied 3p states of sulfur and 4d states of molybdenum, while XES provides complementary information about occupied electronic states that allows monitoring changes in the conduction and valence band of MoS<sub>2</sub> during electrochemical interaction with Na<sup>+</sup> ions. The composition and structure of the initial nanomaterial and the fully discharged electrode were determined using ex situ X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) and X-ray diffraction (XRD). Combined analysis employing surface-sensitive XPS and bulk-sensitive XES and XANES revealed the stability of graphene-coupled MoS<sub>2</sub> layers in SIBs.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="pssb202400272-sec-0002">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="pssb202400272-sec-0002-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="pssb202400272-sec-0003">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="pssb202400272-sec-0003-title">2.1 Structure of MoS<sub>2</sub>-Based Nanomaterial</h3>
            
            <p>MoS<sub>2</sub>-based nanomaterial was synthesized by rapidly heating an aerogel prepared from an aqueous solution of (NH<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>MoS<sub>4</sub> with GO. At a synthesis temperature of 600 °C, (NH<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>MoS<sub>4</sub> decomposes to MoS<sub>2</sub> with the release of NH<sub>3</sub> and H<sub>2</sub>S gases and excess sulfur.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#pssb202400272-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="pssb202400272-bib-0018_R_d128824295e538" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - ACS Appl. Energy Mater.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> GO losses oxygen groups to form rGO layers.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#pssb202400272-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="pssb202400272-bib-0019_R_d128824295e543" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Chem. Phys. Lett.">19</a>, <a href="#pssb202400272-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="pssb202400272-bib-0020_R_d128824295e543" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - J. Mater. Sci.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> A study using scanning electron microscopy (SEM) showed that the synthesis product consists of flakes of crumpled graphene layers (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#pssb202400272-fig-0001">1a</a>). According to transmission electron microscopy (TEM) images, the rGO layers are quite thin because they are transparent and easily wrinkled (Figure <a href="#pssb202400272-fig-0001">1b</a>). The surface of these layers is covered with a porous dark deposit, that is classified as MoS<sub>2</sub> since the electron beam interacts more effectively with elements heavier than carbon.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#pssb202400272-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="pssb202400272-bib-0021_R_d128824295e560" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Phys. Status Solidi B">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> High-resolution TEM images show that the deposit has a layered structure with an interlayer distance of ≈0.62 nm (Figure <a href="#pssb202400272-fig-0001">1c</a>) characteristic of MoS<sub>2</sub>. The number of MoS<sub>2</sub> layers calculated for nonoverlapping particles varies from one to five (some of them are indicated by yellow lines in Figure <a href="#pssb202400272-fig-0001">1c</a>). The Raman spectrum of MoS<sub>2</sub>/rGO powder exhibits sharp peaks at 380.6 and 405.4 cm<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a href="#pssb202400272-fig-0001">1d</a>) corresponding to the E<sup>1</sup><sub>2g</sub> and A<sub>1g</sub> modes, respectively, active in hexagonal 2H-MoS<sub>2</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#pssb202400272-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="pssb202400272-bib-0022_R_d128824295e591" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - C. R. Chime">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The peaks located at ≈1594 and 1363 cm<sup>−1</sup> are attributed to the tangential bond vibrations (G-band) and disorder (D-band) in sp<sup>2</sup>-hybridized carbon, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#pssb202400272-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="pssb202400272-bib-0023_R_d128824295e601" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Phys. Chem. Chem. Phys.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The high intensity ratio <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> of ≈1.04 indicates a large number of defects in the rGO component.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="pssb202400272-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/3a1d5733-50b0-4dde-889d-0a99fdb0bce7/pssb202400272-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/3a1d5733-50b0-4dde-889d-0a99fdb0bce7/pssb202400272-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/5a181de9-63ca-4630-8437-a9a3aa28ba5b/pssb202400272-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/3a1d5733-50b0-4dde-889d-0a99fdb0bce7/pssb202400272-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=pssb202400272-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fpssb.202400272" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) SEM image, b) TEM images taken with low-magnification, c) high-magnification, and d) Raman spectrum of MoS<sub>2</sub>/rGO powder.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>The chemical state of the main elements of the surface of the MoS<sub>2</sub>/rGO sample was determined using XPS. The XPS S 2p spectrum was fitted by four spin-orbit doublets (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#pssb202400272-fig-0002">2a</a>), consisting of 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and 2p<sub>1/2</sub> components with an intensity ratio of 2:1 and separated by ≈1.2 eV.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#pssb202400272-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="pssb202400272-bib-0024_R_d128824295e654" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Surf. Coat. Technol.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The intense doublet with an S 2p<sub>3/2</sub> energy of ≈162.2 eV corresponds to S<sup>2−</sup> from the MoS<sub>2</sub> basal planes, the S 2p<sub>3/2</sub> position at ≈163.2 eV is assigned to the edge dimers S<sub>2</sub><sup>2−</sup>, the high-energy doublet (S 2p<sub>3/2</sub> at ≈168.4 eV) is formed by oxidized sulfur species.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#pssb202400272-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="pssb202400272-bib-0018_R_d128824295e673" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - ACS Appl. Energy Mater.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> A weak low-energy doublet with the S 2p<sub>3/2</sub> component at ≈161.2 eV corresponds to sulfur atoms with a more negative charge than atoms from the basal planes. Such atoms can be located on Mo-edges saturated by 50% with sulfur.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#pssb202400272-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="pssb202400272-bib-0025_R_d128824295e681" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - ACS Nano">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> These four sulfur states were taken into account when fitting the Mo 3d spectrum, which partially overlaps the S 2s binding energy region (Figure <a href="#pssb202400272-fig-0002">2b</a>). The S 2s peak at ≈226.6 eV is mainly contributed by components corresponding to S<sup>2−</sup> and S<sub>2</sub><sup>2−</sup> states. Molybdenum components 3d<sub>5/2</sub> and 3d<sub>3/2</sub> are separated by ≈3.17 eV and have a 3:2 ratio.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#pssb202400272-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="pssb202400272-bib-0024_R_d128824295e699" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Surf. Coat. Technol.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The Mo<sup>4+</sup> states of hexagonal MoS<sub>2</sub> (2H phase) form the dominant doublet in the Mo 3d spectrum of MoS<sub>2</sub>/rGO with the 3d<sub>5/2</sub> component at ≈229.3 eV. The high-energy peak located at ≈235.9 eV indicates the presence of oxidized Mo<sup>6+</sup> states. These states were described by a spin-orbit doublet at ≈232.7/235.9 eV. To correctly reproduce the spectral intensities in the region of ≈230.6 and ≈233.9 eV, an additional doublet was introduced at ≈230.3/233.5 eV, corresponding to the Mo<sup>5+</sup> states. These states may be responsible for bonding the Mo edges with the oxygen groups present in rGO.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#pssb202400272-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="pssb202400272-bib-0026_R_d128824295e717" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Carbon">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Indeed, the XPS C 1s spectrum of the sample showed components located at 287.3 and 289.2 eV (Figure <a href="#pssb202400272-fig-0002">2c</a>) and corresponding to the carbon involved in C–O and C=O bonds, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#pssb202400272-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="pssb202400272-bib-0027_R_d128824295e726" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Nanotechnology">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The main peak at 284.6 eV belongs to sp<sup>2</sup>-hybridized carbon. This peak was described by the Doniach–Šunjić function (full width at half maximum ≈0.99 eV) with an asymmetry parameter of 0.13, which is greater than the corresponding value of 0.095 for the C 1s spectrum of highly oriented pyrolytic graphite.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#pssb202400272-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="pssb202400272-bib-0028_R_d128824295e733" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - ChemCatChem">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The increased peak asymmetry may be due to structural disorder in the wrinkled graphene layers, as well as the interaction of graphene with MoS<sub>2</sub>. Both of these factors influence the delocalization of π electrons and, as a consequence, the core-hole screening effects.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="pssb202400272-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/5c8cc537-14b5-40a9-834c-f740fdca8105/pssb202400272-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/5c8cc537-14b5-40a9-834c-f740fdca8105/pssb202400272-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/561fb949-2654-4309-8f4f-e7795ec972a6/pssb202400272-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/5c8cc537-14b5-40a9-834c-f740fdca8105/pssb202400272-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=pssb202400272-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fpssb.202400272" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) XPS S 2p spectrum, b) Mo 3d spectrum, c) C 1s spectrum measured for MoS<sub>2</sub>/rGO sample. The experimental lines are black and the approximation lines obtained by summing all components are blue.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>Based on the data presented, we conclude that the simultaneous thermolysis of graphene oxide and (NH<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>MoS<sub>4</sub> at a temperature of 600 °C produces a MoS<sub>2</sub>/rGO hybrid nanomaterial in which MoS<sub>2</sub>, consisting of one to five layers, is uniformly distributed along thin graphene layers. According to Raman spectroscopy and XPS data, the MoS<sub>2</sub> layers have a hexagonal structure. Since the preparation of the TEM specimen using ultrasound left MoS<sub>2</sub> layers on the rGO surface, we assume these components interact quite strongly. The high asymmetry of the sp<sup>2</sup> carbon peak and the presence of Mo<sup>5+</sup> states may also be due to interface interactions in the hybrid.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="pssb202400272-sec-0004">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="pssb202400272-sec-0004-title">2.2 Design of Electrochemical Cell</h3>
            
            <p>To measure the X-ray fluorescence emitted by the active electrode, CR2032 coin cells were used. To allow X-rays to penetrate into the cell, the top cap was modified. The main design steps are illustrated in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#pssb202400272-fig-0003">3a</a>. At step 1, a hole with a diameter of 8 mm is cut out on the cap (photo 1 in Figure <a href="#pssb202400272-fig-0003">3b</a>). This size is determined by the 6 × 4 mm<sup>2</sup> X-ray beam spot provided by the VEPP-4M beamline at the “Kosmos” synchrotron source (Novosibirsk, Russia). The hole was covered by 5 μm thick Mylar film (step 2). This film absorbs ≈15% of photons with an energy of ≈2490 eV used to excite the spectra of MoS<sub>2</sub>. In comparison, the absorption of Kapton, often used to create in situ cells transparent for X-rays,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#pssb202400272-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="pssb202400272-bib-0029_R_d128824295e813" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Batter. Supercaps.">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> is ≈44%. A circle of Mylar film with a diameter larger than the diameter of the hole is glued to the back of the cap using high-temperature adhesive. At step 3, a thin layer of Al (≈20 nm) is deposited onto the Mylar film using magnetron sputtering (photo 3 in Figure <a href="#pssb202400272-fig-0003">3b</a>) to ensure the electrical conductivity of the cell. A suspension of electrode material prepared from MoS<sub>2</sub>/rGO, SuperP conductive additive, and polyvinylidene fluoride (PVDF) binder (see <a class="sectionLink scrollableLink" href="#pssb202400272-sec-0008">Experimental Section</a> for details) is spread evenly over the Al/Mylar surface (step 4 in Figure <a href="#pssb202400272-fig-0003">3a</a>) and the electrode is dried in a vacuum. The mass loading of the electrode material is ≈0.5 mg to ensure optimal S K-edge jump. At step 5, a ring of Al foil is placed over the working electrode (photo 5 in Figure <a href="#pssb202400272-fig-0003">3b</a>) to improve contact of the edges of the electrode with the rest of the cell. The resulting top cap is used to assemble the cell using the remaining CR2032 parts, sodium metal, glass fiber separator, and an electrolyte consisting of 1 M NaClO<sub>4</sub> in ethylene carbonate (EC) and dimethyl carbonate (DMC). The voltage of the freshly assembled cell is about 2.3 V. Before measurements, the assembled cells are stored under normal laboratory conditions for 1–3 days. During this time, the cell voltage does not drop, which confirms the successful design of the battery.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="pssb202400272-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/47cc3329-f6b1-42f8-beb0-c01abeb01033/pssb202400272-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/47cc3329-f6b1-42f8-beb0-c01abeb01033/pssb202400272-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/4da76f61-69e1-404a-98c1-9cd9f0d3b708/pssb202400272-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/47cc3329-f6b1-42f8-beb0-c01abeb01033/pssb202400272-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=pssb202400272-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fpssb.202400272" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Sequence of modifying the top cell cap and applying the electrode material. b) Photographs of the back of the cell cap at some manufacturing steps: 1 – hole in the center of the cap, 3 – Al-coated Mylar film covering the hole, and 5 – electrode material on Mylar film with a foil ring on top.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>To test the applicability of the designed cell for recording XES and XANES spectra of sulfur and molybdenum, we compared the spectra of the MoS<sub>2</sub>/rGO powder and this nanomaterial as part of the electrode material located in the cell. The metallic Na, which is a component of our half-cell, is highly reactive and can easily interact with the liquid electrolyte even during battery assembly.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#pssb202400272-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="pssb202400272-bib-0030_R_d128824295e863" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - J. Energy Chem.">30</a>, <a href="#pssb202400272-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="pssb202400272-bib-0031_R_d128824295e863" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Energy Environ. Sci.">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The resulting species can diffuse to another electrode and react with it. Sulfur atoms are most accessible in the MoS<sub>2</sub>/rGO electrode because these atoms are located on either side of the molybdenum plane in the MoS<sub>2</sub> layers. Therefore, we first examined the electronic state of sulfur.</p>
            
            <p>
               <b>Figure</b> <a href="#pssb202400272-fig-0004">4a</a> shows the XES S Kα<sub>1,2</sub> spectra, resulting from the filling of holes created on the core S 1s levels with electrons from occupied 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and 2p<sub>1/2</sub> levels. The spectrum of the MoS<sub>2</sub>/rGO powder is dominated by a spin-orbit doublet with the Kα<sub>1</sub> emission energy of ≈2307.4 eV, corresponding to the S<sup>2−</sup> state in MoS<sub>2</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#pssb202400272-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="pssb202400272-bib-0032_R_d128824295e896" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - J. Anal. At. Spectrom.">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The sample probing depth is several micrometers for XES and several nanometers for XPS. Considering that the XES intensity of the high-energy doublet from the oxidized states of sulfur (S Kα<sub>1</sub> at ≈2308.6 eV) is insignificant, and the intensity of the corresponding component in the XPS spectrum is noticeable (Figure <a href="#pssb202400272-fig-0002">2a</a>), we conclude that SO<sub><i>x</i></sub> species should be located predominantly on the MoS<sub>2</sub>/rGO surface. The fraction of oxidized sulfur species slightly increases in the MoS<sub>2</sub>/rGO electrode (2.3 V cell), as evidenced by the growth of the corresponding doublet in the battery spectrum.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="pssb202400272-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/1ed096c9-79ea-41e9-9920-131355d62f61/pssb202400272-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/1ed096c9-79ea-41e9-9920-131355d62f61/pssb202400272-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/421afb06-6faa-4790-85a7-c7bc824ae6dd/pssb202400272-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/1ed096c9-79ea-41e9-9920-131355d62f61/pssb202400272-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=pssb202400272-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fpssb.202400272" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Comparison of X-ray spectra of MoS<sub>2</sub>/rGO powder (orange) and MoS<sub>2</sub>/rGO electrode in a sodium-ion half-cell at 2.3 V (black): a) XES S Kα<sub>1,2</sub> spectra, blue lines are obtained by summing the components; b) XANES S K-edge spectra and c) Mo L<sub>3</sub>-edge spectra in the energy range from 2519 to 2533 eV. Inset in (c) shows the full Mo L<sub>3</sub>-edge spectra. The black solid lines in (b, c) are smoothing of the measurement data for the cell.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>Figure <a href="#pssb202400272-fig-0004">4b</a> compares the normalized XANES spectra measured at the S K-edge of the samples. The differences in spectral intensities may be due to the different photon registration modes used for the MoS<sub>2</sub>/rGO powder (transmission mode) and the MoS<sub>2</sub>/rGO electrode material in the cell (fluorescence yield mode). Both spectra show an intense peak at ≈2470.5 eV, corresponding to S<sup>2−</sup> in MoS<sub>2</sub>, and a weaker peak at ≈2482.1 eV from oxidized forms of sulfur.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#pssb202400272-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="pssb202400272-bib-0033_R_d128824295e964" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Adv. Sci.">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The S K-edge spectrum arises from 1s → 3p electron transitions. At high-oxidation states of sulfur, the 3p orbitals are empty and overlap with the O 2p orbitals to form the SO<sub><i>x</i></sub> peak. In sulfides, the portion of empty S 3p states depends on hybridization with metal orbitals.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#pssb202400272-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="pssb202400272-bib-0034_R_d128824295e972" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Anal. Chem.">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, the intensity of the SO<sub><i>x</i></sub> peak in the S K-edge spectrum of sulfides is not a quantitate measure of the oxidized sulfur content of the sample. To assess the difference in the degree of oxidation between the two studied samples, we used the ratio of the intensity of the SO<sub><i>x</i></sub> peak to the intensity of the main S<sup>2−</sup> peak. The ratio changes from 0.40 in the powder spectrum to 0.43 in the cell spectrum, indicating a slight increase in sulfur oxidation in the latter case, consistent with XES observation.</p>
            
            <p>The XANES Mo L<sub>3</sub>-edge spectrum gives information about the density of molybdenum 4d states. Comparison of the spectra of powder and electrochemical cell reveals a clear shift of the adsorption edge by ≈0.3 eV to low energies in the latter case (Figure <a href="#pssb202400272-fig-0004">4c</a>). Therefore, contact of MoS<sub>2</sub>/rGO with the components of the electrochemical cell affects the Mo 4d states.</p>
            
            <p>Summarizing this part, an electrochemical cell containing a liquid electrolyte was successfully designed for measuring X-ray spectra. According to XES and XANES data, metallic sodium and NaClO<sub>4</sub>/EC/DMC electrolyte present in a cell with a MoS<sub>2</sub>/rGO working electrode cause partial oxidation of sulfur and a shift of unoccupied Mo 4d states to low energies.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="pssb202400272-sec-0005">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="pssb202400272-sec-0005-title">2.3 Operando and In Situ X-Ray Spectroscopy Measurements</h3>
            
            <p>The first discharge curve of a cell with a transparent window is shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#pssb202400272-fig-0005">5a</a>. The plateau at ≈0.9 V versus Na<sup>+</sup>/Na corresponds to the insertion of sodium ions into the interlayer space of MoS<sub>2</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#pssb202400272-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="pssb202400272-bib-0006_R_d128824295e1022" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - ACS Nano">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The circles on the curve indicate the cell voltage during measurements of the XANES spectra.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="pssb202400272-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/641b7c48-f1a4-4693-854f-eb2bfd26544d/pssb202400272-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/641b7c48-f1a4-4693-854f-eb2bfd26544d/pssb202400272-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/1bd9271c-4d68-4f55-aa4d-76753d0756f9/pssb202400272-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/641b7c48-f1a4-4693-854f-eb2bfd26544d/pssb202400272-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=pssb202400272-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fpssb.202400272" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) First discharge curve of sodium half-cell with the MoS<sub>2</sub>/rGO electrode and a Mylar window in the top cap recorded at a current density of 0.1 A.g<sup>−1</sup>. The circles identify cell voltages during XANES measurements. b) S K-edge and c) Mo L<sub>3</sub>-edge spectra recorded for the cell at various voltages are shown as thin lines; thick lines are smoothed measurement data. Insets in (c) show photos of the assembled cell with a window (bottom) and the cell after measuring the X-ray spectra (top).</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>Storing the cell in air for 3 weeks causes the voltage to drop to ≈0.7 eV due to self-discharge occurring at open circuit potential.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#pssb202400272-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="pssb202400272-bib-0035_R_d128824295e1061" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Energy Storage Mater.">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Comparison of the XANES spectra of a fresh cell with a voltage of 2.3 V and the same cell with a voltage reduced to 0.7 V as a result of a three-week pause between the measurements shows a significant difference in the S K-edge spectra (Figure <a href="#pssb202400272-fig-0005">5b</a>) and similarity of the Mo L<sub>3</sub>-edge spectra (Figure <a href="#pssb202400272-fig-0005">5c</a>). Self-discharge of the cell is accompanied by a decrease in the relative intensity of the peak at 2471 eV of the S K-edge spectrum, responsible for the hybridization of the S 3p and Mo 4d states, and a growth of the SO<sub><i>x</i></sub> peak. As compared to the fresh cell, the Mo L<sub>3</sub>-edge of the stored cell exhibits an increase in intensity at 2526.6 eV, which is associated with MoO<sub>x</sub> states.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#pssb202400272-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="pssb202400272-bib-0033_R_d128824295e1082" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Adv. Sci.">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Nuclear magnetic resonance (NMR) examination of NaClO<sub>4</sub>/EC/DMC electrolyte contacted with a Na metal electrode in a glove box for 10 days at room temperature revealed the formation of sodium methyl carbonate and sodium propyl carbonate.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#pssb202400272-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="pssb202400272-bib-0036_R_d128824295e1090" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - J. Power Sources">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Sodium alkyl carbonates promote decomposition of ClO<sub>4</sub><sup>−</sup> anion, and the resulting highly reactive peroxyl radicals can react with sulfur and molybdenum located at the edges of MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets. Since the intensity of the SO<sub><i>x</i></sub> peak (Figure <a href="#pssb202400272-fig-0005">5b</a>) increased more significantly than the intensity of the MoO<sub>x</sub> peak (Figure <a href="#pssb202400272-fig-0005">5c</a>) we conclude that oxygen is preferably added to sulfur.</p>
            
            <p>The following XANES experiments were carried out for the cell connected to a potentiostat, that is, in operando conditions. The cell with a residual voltage of 0.7 V was actively discharged to 0.6 V and after recording the spectra, deeply discharged to 0.1 V. The relative intensities of the main peak located around 2471 eV in the S K-edge spectra for these two samples decreased as compared to the 0.7 V cell stored at open circuit potential (Figure <a href="#pssb202400272-fig-0005">5b</a>). This may indicate the population of the initially empty 3p states of sulfur with sodium electrons as a result of the electrochemical interaction of MoS<sub>2</sub> and Na<sup>+</sup> ions. This interaction also affects the 4d states of molybdenum, since the intensity of the Mo L<sub>3</sub>-edge decreases significantly for the 0.1 V cell (Figure <a href="#pssb202400272-fig-0005">5c</a>). The position of the main peak in the S K- and Mo L<sub>3</sub>-edge spectra shifts by ≈0.3 eV toward lower energies when the cell is discharged from 0.6 to 0.1 V. The same energy shift for both spectra indicates that the charge transferred from the intercalated sodium is evenly distributed on sulfur and molybdenum. Interestingly, the intensity ratio of the SO<sub>x</sub> peak to the main MoS<sub>2</sub> peak in the S K-edge spectra of the sample discharged to 0.1 V is ≈0.9 times lower than that in the spectrum of the 0.7 V cell (Figure <a href="#pssb202400272-fig-0005">5b</a>). Discharging the cell using a potentiostat accelerates the decomposition of the electrolyte and the products form a solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) layer on the MoS<sub>2</sub>/rGO electrode. It is likely that some of the previously formed SO<sub>x</sub> species are involved in this process.</p>
            
            <p>XES measurements were carried out on a cell that was discharged to 1.5 V or 0 V using a potentiostat and then transferred into the vacuum chamber of the X-ray spectrometer to excite the S Kα<sub>1,2</sub> and Mo Lα<sub>1,2</sub> spectra. The S Kα<sub>1,2</sub> spectra of the discharged cell consist of two doublets (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#pssb202400272-fig-0006">6a</a>) corresponding to S<sup>2−</sup> in MoS<sub>2</sub> (S Kα<sub>1</sub> at 2307.4 eV) and more oxidized sulfur states (S Kα<sub>1</sub> at 2308.6 eV). The relative intensity of the high-energy doublet in the spectrum of the cell at 1.5 V is significantly higher than in the spectrum of the freshly assembled cell (Figure <a href="#pssb202400272-fig-0004">4a</a>). The contribution from oxidized sulfur species to the spectrum increases for the fully discharged cell (0 V). As we discussed previously, oxygen-containing radicals formed in the sodium-ion half-cell can be responsible for the formation of SO<sub><i>x</i></sub> species on the MoS<sub>2</sub> surface. The fact that the fraction of these species increases as the cell is discharged from 1.5 to 0 V indicates that the decomposition of the electrolyte continues in this voltage range. The position of the S Kα<sub>1,2</sub> spectrum components does not depend on the cell voltage. In contrast, the Mo Lα<sub>1,2</sub> peaks downshift by ≈0.4 eV as the cell discharges from 1.5 to 0 V (Figure <a href="#pssb202400272-fig-0006">6b</a>). The XES Mo Lα<sub>1,2</sub> spectrum arises as a result of the electron transitions from 3d<sub>5/2</sub> and 3d<sub>3/2</sub> levels to holes at the 2p<sub>3/2</sub> levels. The 2p states are also involved in the formation of the Mo L<sub>3</sub>-edge spectrum, which exhibits a 0.3 eV downshift as the cell discharges from 0.6 to 0.1 V (Figure <a href="#pssb202400272-fig-0005">5c</a>). The decrease in the energy of the core levels indicates the reduction of molybdenum, which is most likely due to receiving the electron density from sodium.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="pssb202400272-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/561d9e9b-0df0-4824-8060-cc4c14828cf1/pssb202400272-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/561d9e9b-0df0-4824-8060-cc4c14828cf1/pssb202400272-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/017bfb47-1b62-4fe9-b8ae-28503d3c115b/pssb202400272-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/561d9e9b-0df0-4824-8060-cc4c14828cf1/pssb202400272-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=pssb202400272-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fpssb.202400272" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) XES S Kα<sub>1,2</sub> spectra and b) Mo Lα<sub>1,2</sub> spectra, measured for the cell discharged to 1.5 V (top) and 0 V (bottom). The experimental lines are black and the approximation lines obtained by summing all components are blue.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>Thus, a combination of ex situ XES and operando XANES measurements of actively discharged cells with the MoS<sub>2</sub>/rGO electrode showed that the additional electron density caused by intercalating Na<sup>+</sup> ions in MoS<sub>2</sub> is located predominantly at Mo atoms. This conclusion follows from the shift of the Mo Lα<sub>1,2</sub> line toward lower energies for the completely discharged cell and at the same time, the absence of a change in the position of the S<sup>2−</sup> doublet in the S Kα<sub>1,2</sub> spectrum. The low-energy shifts of the main MoS<sub>2</sub> peaks observed in the S K-edge and Mo L<sub>3</sub>-edge spectra upon battery discharge from 0.6 to 0.1 V versus Na<sup>+</sup>/Na may be associated with changes in the local environment of sulfur and molybdenum. The S K-edge and S Kα<sub>1,2</sub> spectra revealed an increase in the fraction of SO<sub>x</sub> species during self-discharge and active discharge of the battery. We attribute this increase to oxygen-containing radicals that can form upon reaction of Na metal with the NaClO<sub>4</sub>/EC/DMC electrolyte.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#pssb202400272-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="pssb202400272-bib-0036_R_d128824295e1253" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - J. Power Sources">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Note that the design of our cell allows it to be used for measurements within 1–3 days. The insets in Figure <a href="#pssb202400272-fig-0005">5c</a> demonstrate that the battery remains intact after prolonged exposure to X-rays under vacuum conditions.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="pssb202400272-sec-0006">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="pssb202400272-sec-0006-title">2.4 Ex situ XPS and XRD Study of Electrode</h3>
            
            <p>To determine changes in the composition of MoS<sub>2</sub>/rGO as a result of the first sodium insertion, the electrode was examined after the cell was fully discharged. Before the XPS experiment, the electrode material removed from the cell was washed with diethyl carbonate (DEC), dried in a glove box and transferred into the X-ray spectrometer chamber, avoiding contact with air.</p>
            
            <p>The working electrode material consists of MoS<sub>2</sub>/rGO mixed with carbon black SuperP and PVDF. A comparison of the XPS C 1s spectra of the initial electrode and the electrode after the first discharge reveals a broadening of the main C–C component in the latter spectrum and a strong increase in the intensity of high-energy components corresponding to carbon bound to foreign elements (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#pssb202400272-fig-0007">7a</a>). These changes are due to the formation of a SEI layer on the surface of the electrode material. In this layer, carbon atoms are involved in the groups H<sub>2</sub>C–O– (component at ≈286.3 eV), alkyls carbonates (component ROCO<sub>2</sub><sup>−</sup>at ≈288.5 eV), and carbonate (component CO<sub>3</sub><sup>2−</sup> at ≈290.4 eV).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#pssb202400272-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="pssb202400272-bib-0037_R_d128824295e1292" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Nano Energy">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The XPS Cl 2p spectrum of the discharged electrode consists of two intense doublets (Figure <a href="#pssb202400272-fig-0007">7b</a>). The binding energy of the Cl 2p<sub>3/2</sub> component ≈198.8 eV corresponds to NaCl;<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#pssb202400272-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="pssb202400272-bib-0037_R_d128824295e1303" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Nano Energy">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> the Cl 2p<sub>3/2</sub> component located at ≈200.1 eV can be attributed to covalent C–Cl bonds.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#pssb202400272-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="pssb202400272-bib-0038_R_d128824295e1310" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Phys. Status Solidi B">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> These bonds are formed as a result of the electrochemical reduction of NaClO<sub>4</sub> and the interaction of chlorine with carbon present in SEI. The high-energy doublet ClO<sub>4</sub><sup>−</sup> is very weak. The XPS Na 1s spectrum are fitted by two components (Figure <a href="#pssb202400272-fig-0007">7c</a>). The intense component at 1071.8 eV is associated with NaCl and Na<sup>+</sup> ions intercalated in MoS<sub>2</sub>; the weaker component at 1073.2 eV is due to Na<sup>+</sup> bound to anions CO<sub>3</sub><sup>2−</sup> and ROCO<sub>2</sub><sup>−</sup>.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="pssb202400272-fig-0007"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/e6123660-4965-4ab4-bb25-c3e0e44c3d61/pssb202400272-fig-0007-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/e6123660-4965-4ab4-bb25-c3e0e44c3d61/pssb202400272-fig-0007-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/e0dce1ae-507d-4e12-abd0-265db3b13f18/pssb202400272-fig-0007-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/e6123660-4965-4ab4-bb25-c3e0e44c3d61/pssb202400272-fig-0007-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 7<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=pssb202400272-fig-0007&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fpssb.202400272" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
XPS spectra: a) C 1s spectra of initial electrode material (1) and the electrode after the first discharge (2), b) Cl 2p spectrum and c) Na 1s spectrum of the discharged electrode material. The experimental lines are black and the approximation lines obtained by summing all components are blue.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>According to survey XPS spectra (not shown), the Mo/C ratio in the discharge electrode decreases by approximately three times as compared to that for the initial electrode. The inelastic mean free path (IMFP) for Mo 3d electrons with a kinetic energy of 1250 eV (for an Al Kα X-ray source) is ≈3.4 nm for carbon materials,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#pssb202400272-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="pssb202400272-bib-0039_R_d128824295e1363" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Surf. Interface Anal.">39</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> resulting in an estimated SEI layer thickness of ≈3.7 nm.</p>
            
            <p>XPS S 2p and Mo 3d spectra of the discharged MoS<sub>2</sub>/rGO electrode are shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#pssb202400272-fig-0008">8a,b</a>, respectively. In the fitting procedure we used components identified from the analysis of the corresponding spectra of MoS<sub>2</sub>/rGO powder (Figure <a href="#pssb202400272-fig-0002">2a,b</a>). The SO<sub>x</sub> doublet in the S 2p spectrum of the electrode is shifted by ≈0.1 eV relative to the position of this doublet in the spectrum of the powder. Using this as a reference, the binding energies of the doublets corresponding to S<sup>2−</sup> from the basal planes of the 2H-MoS<sub>2</sub> and S<sub>2</sub><sup>2−</sup> dimers were increased by 0.1 eV (<b>Table</b> <a class="tableLink scrollableLink" title="Link to table" href="#pssb202400272-tbl-0001">1</a>). To describe the experimental S 2p spectral line, a low-energy doublet at ≈161.2/162.4 eV was added (Figure <a href="#pssb202400272-fig-0008">8a</a>). The same action was applied to approximate the Mo 3d spectrum of the electrode. The spectral intensity remaining in the low-energy region after the introduction of doublets corresponding to the states Mo<sup>6+</sup>, Mo<sup>5+</sup>, and Mo<sup>4+</sup> from 2H-MoS<sub>2</sub> was described by a doublet with a binding energy of ≈228.0/231.2 eV (Figure <a href="#pssb202400272-fig-0008">8b</a>). As compared to the MoS<sub>2</sub>/rGO powder, the XPS spectra of the discharged electrode showed an increase in the intensity of SO<sub>x</sub> and Mo<sup>6+</sup> doublets corresponding to the oxidized states of sulfur and molybdenum and a shift of the latter doublet toward lower energies (Table <a class="tableLink scrollableLink" title="Link to table" href="#pssb202400272-tbl-0001">1</a>). This observation is consistent with XANES and XES data and is explained by the oxidation of the edge atoms of MoS<sub>2</sub> sheets by reactive products of the electrolyte decomposition.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="pssb202400272-fig-0008"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/24eb0615-57f3-4abd-8c29-05276a01b393/pssb202400272-fig-0008-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/24eb0615-57f3-4abd-8c29-05276a01b393/pssb202400272-fig-0008-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/34229c34-9aea-4fca-a93c-37ba0c1c85a3/pssb202400272-fig-0008-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/24eb0615-57f3-4abd-8c29-05276a01b393/pssb202400272-fig-0008-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 8<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=pssb202400272-fig-0008&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fpssb.202400272" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) XPS S 2p and b) Mo 3d spectra of the MoS<sub>2</sub>/rGO electrode extracted from the cell after the first full discharge. The experimental lines are black and the approximation lines obtained by summing all components are blue. c) Comparison of XRD patterns of (1) MoS<sub>2</sub>/rGO powder and (2) MoS<sub>2</sub>/rGO electrode after the first discharge. The asterisk corresponds to the reflection of PVDF.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <div class="article-table-content" id="pssb202400272-tbl-0001">
